		<username>tomgillespie2002</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Powerful damning of the American justice system</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on Robert Elliot Burns' autobiographical novel I Am a Fugitive from a Georgia Chain Gang!, Mervyn LeRoy's powerful drama follows war veteran James Allen (Paul Muni) who starts life as a drifter to pursue his dream of engineering. Out of work and broke, he is tempted into a free burger at a diner by a fellow drifter, only to be caught up in a robbery, to which he is forced into participation. He is arrested, and sentenced to ten years on the Georgia chain gang, where he and his fellow jailbirds are routinely mistreated and occasionally flogged. He manages to escape, and after a short spell as a fugitive, manages to work his way up in an construction firm. Years later, having established himself as a prominent member of society, he is finally tracked down. Unwilling to turn him over state lines, his new city Chicago becomes embroiled in a battle of custody with Georgia, who want to return him to the chain gang for the remainder of his sentence.Not merely a generic prison movie, I am a Fugitive... finds its power rooted in it's condemnation of the American justice system. It is claimed that Burns' experiences and eventual exposure of the barbaric nature of the chain gang led to its cancellation. These men work bare- backed in the blazing sun, hammering rocks and train lines and whatever else there is to strike, eating gruel unfit for an animal in between. These are hardened criminals (apart from Allen, who is certainly innocent, but whether Burns truly was is another question), so do they deserve a harsh punishment? Of course. Do they deserve to have their human rights taken away from them and to be treated like dogs? Certainly not. But it goes deeper than that, and, anchored by Muni's terrific performance, questions how a person can ever prove themselves fit for society. Allen becomes a pillar of his community, surely an indication of rehabilitation? And is the way to help a person get to this stage to beat them down at every turn, diminishing all sense of hope?This being pre-Hays Code, LeRoy is allowed to take the movie to places you wouldn't stand a chance of seeing just a few years later. Allen enjoys the comfort of a prostitute, looking up and down her body with an animalistic look in his eyes, adding depth to a complex character, taking his arc from squeaky-clean optimist to flawed, damaged human being. Special mention must go to cinematographer Sol Polito, who captures the sweaty claustrophobia of the chain gang, as well as framing a rather savage flogging scene with Expressionist flair, giant shadows creeping up the walls. I also ask you to try and avoid squirming during the scene in which Allen has his shackles purposefully dented for his escape, the mallet crashing down on his ankle repeatedly. 81 years after its release, Fugitive still packs a considerable punch, highlighting a heavily flawed justice system that hasn't really learned from its mistakes (see the incredible Paradise Lost (1996-2011) documentaries for proof).www.the-wrath-of-blog.blogspot.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Rahwayrunner</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>B&amp;W drama, good example of early sound pictures</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I am a Fugitive of the Chain Gang" is a black and white drama set in the 1930's. Directed by Mervyn LeRoy and released in 1932. Based on an autobiography, Paul Muni stars as James Allen, a World War I veteran who has trouble returning to civilian life. Travelling the US during the start of the depression era, James Allen is caught up in an ill-fated attempt to rod a diner. His prison sentence results in confinement with a Georgia chain gang. Rather than face years in the work camp, Muni's character escapes the chain gang, and builds a successful career. However, he is haunted by the prospect of someone finding out about his past. An early sound picture, most of the picture is filled dialogue and little musical score. Rather than fade to black, scene transitions are accompanied by a variety of sound effects. For example, the trial scene ends with the judge's gavel pounding, and then segues into the sound of pick axes hammering rocks as we see the chain gang working on a new railroad bed. The movie has several similar transitions, and makes effective use of many unusual sounds throughout the picture. The total run time is 92 minutes.</fullReview>

		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A great piece of 1930's social commentary</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang is a 1932 prison drama about a man who keeps ending up in the wrong place at the wrong time. This is an excellent movie. Most notably for its use of visuals and sounds, for example: when illustrating time gone by, there will be a cut to a calender, or a pay stub with the date on it. I don't know if this is the first use of such visuals in a movie but its the earliest such use I've ever seen. The movie also uses audio in very smart and clever ways. Like when Muni's character gets whipped for talking off to the prison guards, you don't actually see him get whipped, you just hear the cracking of the whip, which i think is worse. It allows for your imagination to step in and makes the scene harder to watch. This film is also a great piece of social commentary. The story and the protagonist is based on a real person. I think that's the most jarring part of this movie, that people actually had to live like that. Weather they deserved it or not is up to the audience, but it is important for people to know how 'criminals' get treated, and this movie pulls that off perfectly. Overall this is a solid movie, it joins the ranks of Metropolis, Citizens Kane, Taxi Driver, and Blade Runner as a movie that was way ahead of its time. 9/10</fullReview>

As one of the early sound films, director Mervyn LeRoy uses stylized editing to draw the viewer's attention to the use of sound in the film. Sound effects are often used as transitions or as indicators of what will happen in the next scene. Dialogue is almost never used at the same time as the sounds. This effect is not overwhelming, however: it adds a sense of urgency to the film that helps the viewer sympathize with Allen.</fullReview>

		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A groundbreaking film from Mervyn Leroy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Although 80 years have passed since the premiere of this film it still remains one of the most smashing, distressing and harrowing depictions of prison violence in history. The story of James Allen (brilliantly and believably played by the up-and-coming Paul Muni) is both a significant, not-so-typical cautionary tale and a brave statement against inane jail systems and covert human exploitation. Upon his return home James - contrary to his family's requests - rejects the job offer from his earlier employer and decides to follow the dream of becoming a prominent architect. Unfortunately, the country's economic instability shows its true face when James rides around the country without any chances of finding real work. If this wasn't enough, one day he meets a shady character who invites him for a free burger at a nearby joint. Soon it becomes clear that this man is actually a small-time robber, and James is dragged into the whole intrigue with him, just to be found guilty with a proper trail and sentenced for 10 years on a chain gang. The enormous brutality and inhumane treatment of the prisoners make I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang a real punch-in-the-face. One day James finally finds a way of escaping from the nightmare and begins to re-live his life and finally fulfills his lifelong dream. While he soon becomes a master craftsman in the architectural industry, new problems arise thereafter. He is pressured into marrying his landlady - who happens to know his past - and when he begins to have feelings for another woman, she starts threatening his huge secret. Before long he is once again - this time voluntarily, due to his prior agreement with the prison chieftains - sentenced to hard work in the chain gang. Hoping for a one-month stay he endures another 10 years in the horrible, atrocious conditions. I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang is a perfectly astounding expose of the unstable and literally bestial chain gang system. With its sinister ambiance and hard-hitting imagery, the film presents a grievous story of one innocent man's attempt at rescuing what's left of his already dilapidated life.</fullReview>

Films like this are almost mandatory watching to make sure tragedies like this don't happen again.</fullReview>

		<username>chaos-rampant</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>10 Years in Hell</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I keep harping on about abstraction in film, cultivating an abstract eye. This is not theoretical gymnastics from my view, quite the opposite. The point is that life, modern life as you and I know it now or as people did back in the Depression, is incessantly complicated and yields disappointment and suffering at every turn, it is not some ancestral sin that does this mind you, it only happens because the mind is built to compute that way. So we want to see clearly in all of this, why it is so, and preferably by illustrating examples in film.We could plainly swim across a river or find trees to forage on this side, but find it best to build bridges like the protagonist is driven to here. We're driven beings. We strive for crafty solutions to the universe. We are satisfied by complex narratives. These have to be complicated because the universe is, and building the first spears taught us to survive this way.So it has all been a conflict between the human engineer striving for complicated order and unseen forces meddling with the plans. But things will break down, sometimes for no apparent reason at all. Fate is only this notion we have invented where things break but according to higher plan and there was never any choice in the matter.In an indirect way this is correct, insofar as the universe regroups itself through spontaneous chaos, but this being part of the plan is difficult to sensibly communicate in the human level, in a way that will make us want to build anything at all to begin with. So we settle for bogus fate. In the East they have made their peace with that long ago and proof of that is their much more refined notion of fate, but they're busy these days learning from us otherwise.So this is what we have here, a film about a man fresh from chaos - World War I - who wants to create and build things that matter, yet being continuously foiled in his enterprise by callous fates. This brings him, among other stops, washed out in a barren limbo as a chain-gang member breaking rocks from sunup to sunset.He's only somewhat in control of swooping from one world to the next, prison to civilian life and back again, but each new world he enters is already preordained and carries remembrances of the ones before, usually some form of mindless laboring. He's only content when designing structures, providing plan.If the shots of chain-gang members breaking rocks bring to mind the statuesque vigor of happy worker-bees in Soviet films, the comparison is apt. The Soviets had got this part right, that you begin a world with breaking rocks, notice also in the film how simply content is the black to do what he must and always hits the nail on its head, proof of relaxed mind, but you also have to have freedom and ambition, a dream like the Americans had.So because we cannot be fully in control of dreams we have about bridging across to something and life keeps breaking down on us, remember the film was made for audiences of the Depression, we have endless karmic returns and all the misery and despair that go with living in cycles.We may contend ourselves that it was some blind crazy god who pushed him to the wrong diner at the wrong moment for a bet, but this does not bring us anywhere. Was it another god that got him out of the Belgian dugouts alive? No, sometimes you just have to bite the bullet and do the nine months in hell.So there you go, the third missing link of film noir. Dr. Mabuse had covered storytelling manipulation of reality. Blue Angel gave us the femme fatale, the staged dream of her. To my knowledge, this is the first film that presents such a despairing vision of an ordinary schmuck being crushed helpless in cosmic cogs.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2012-06-07</date>
		<username>Cosmoeticadotcom (cosmoetica@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Good</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Like James Cagney, Paul Muni was a Broadway star who made it big in Hollywood, during the early sound era. And, like Cagney, his breakthrough role was that of a gangster, in a Warner Brothers film. In Cagney's case, it was in 1931's The Public Enemy, and in Muni's case it was a year later, in the original Scarface (yes, this was the film that the 1980s Al Pacino quasi-comedy was loosely based on). Later, that same year, Muni delivered his second powerhouse performance, in another black and white Warner Brothers social crime drama: I Am A Fugitive From A Chain Gang, directed by Mervyn Leroy, who was coming off the successful Little Caesar, which made Edward G. Robinson a star. Of the stars of the Big Three Gangster films of that era, only Muni totally escaped the shadow of gangster typecasting, but that did not mean he could not do a crime drama about another subject entirely. But, before I move on, let me return to the Cagney comparison, for while their career trajectories toward stardom were fairly similar, they also succeeded because of another similarity: both men were great actors whose styles were far more 'real' than the overt acting styles that were carryovers from the silent days, and would not be relinquished until the 1950s, when, first Neo-Realism, then the New Wave, changed film forever. And this was, like Cagney, in large part to being a Broadway and Vaudeville star before hitting film. Another similarity the two actors shared was an ability to dominate the screen. Cagney did so with sheer brute magnetism. He was, despite any roles, always JIMMY CAGNEY! Muni, on the other hand, was a sort of Lon Chaney who could transcend horror genres. Muni's face was like clay, whether in makeup or not, and one could read his characters' thoughts just by watching his facial reactions and the glint in his eyes. Simply one of the best actors in film in any era, and a good quarter century ahead of the curve in acting, in terms of realism.I Am A Fugitive From A Chain Gang is also a very good film, near-great. The only thing that holds it back from true greatness are a few plot holes and some weak acting from the supporting cast. It's greatest quality, though, is how relevant and undated a film it is re: race, injustice, evil, society, the so-called 'justice system,' etc.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2012-03-07</date>
		<username>kevin_m_sullivan</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Thank God For Sound</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Prior to seeing I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang, I had never heard of Paul Muni, and I have no idea how. His portrayal of James Allen, a wrongfully convicted "burglar," and his transformation into Allen James, a revered construction foreman, is inspiring. Watching this film after having seen several silents, I was thankful I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang was made after sound truly entered the mix in film making. There were several powerful scenes that would have been less dramatic (and thus, less impacting) if there were no audio components. One such scene depicts the group of prisoners being woken up by the sound of a chain running through their shackles. The clanking of the chain overpowers any other noise, which powerfully signifies the prisoners social and physical disabilities. The film also uses sound to juxtapose freedom and imprisonment. During his time in the chain gang, the film is full of harsh noises and yelling. As a free man, Allen's life is much quieter. Aside from being a great drama, the film serves as a sort of social commentary. It draws attention to severe injustices issued by our nations judicial system, and does a great job doing so.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>The film "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" directed by Mervyn LeRoy is an excellent film and a classic example of a suspense filled plot. For example, whenever the prisoners are moving in the chain gang, the sound syncs almost perfectly to the movements of prisoners. 		<shortDescription>Slow to Start, But Worth the Wait</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I Am a Fugitive From a Chain Gang" is a great studio era drama, and features another impressive performance by Paul Muni (though his role as Tony Camonte in Scarface is admittedly hard to beat). Here, he is in a very different, far more sympathetic role, as a wrongfully convicted felon. One of this films greatest attributes is it's look and feel. The sets are well chosen, and help to make the film fairly realistic for it's time. The deplorable conditions of life on a a chain gang are emphasized heavily by this and also in the films use of sound; the constant clanging of pick axes's and hammers are maddening, and the singing of the convicts sounds absolutely hopeless. Even the sound of the hammer knocking away the calendar months to me sounded somewhat like the toll of a clock tower, sounding off the passage of time that, for the convicts, is essentially meaningless. Overall, I thought that this film was great, but I did think it had a relatively slow beginning.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Excellent job Mr. Muni</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I found the movie I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang to be a highly effective and well made movie. Using the technology available at the time, (1932), it told a compelling story in a way that was moving and unforgettable.The plot was suspenseful and fast paced. It was also fill with a sad and disturbing irony. The protagonist, James Allen, is a WW1 vet who returns from the war and wants to go out on his own, be his own person, and build bridges and his own life. Instead, he's tricked into taking the blame for a crime he didn't commit and ends up serving on a chain gang. He endures unimaginably harsh treatment, survives and escapes. He then builds a good life for himself, only to be turned in by the authorities by his wife. He then gets duped into returning to prison again for what will be a very long time. He escapes again, fleeing as a broken man with no hope for the sort of life he wanted, a victim of circumstances he could never control and a social and justice system that seem intent on destroying him.Muni's acting was excellent. He made the character of James Allen both believable and sympathetic. In doing so , Muni made the movie even better because it helped the viewer care about Allen and hope that he'd somehow survive and have a good life.Adding to the effectiveness of the movie was the sound. It was new technology at the time, so it didn't always match up with the visual images, but overall, it did a good job and added to the story.The final element that I believe enhanced the story line was the fact that it was in black and white. While it was all that was available at the time, I think it was perfect for the dark and brooding world that Allen inhabited.</fullReview>

		<username>Hwos</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Gritty Hard Luck Story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is a look at the dark reality of James Allen. Allen, played perfectly by Paul Muni, is a decorated veteran from World War I that returns from the war a changed man. He isn't happy returning to his mundane life, so he strikes out to follow his passion, engineering. Life on the road, looking for work is tough on Allen and leads him into a bad situation. He is the unwitting accomplice in an armed robbery, and gets arrested, convicted, and sentenced to a hard labor prison camp despite his innocence. The film does a wonderful job of conveying the brutality and desperation of Allen's life as a prisoner, with great lighting and sound effects. Against all odds Allen narrowly escapes the chain gang, and starts a new life. He follows his love for engineering, turns his life around, and becomes a great man in Chicago. Unfortunately for Allen his troubled past is always lurking just around the corner, and it eventually catches up to him. He is exposed by his wife to authorities and arrested as a fugitive. His stature as a businessman affords him the right to negotiate a conditional surrender and he returns to the prison camp to serve a symbolic portion of his sentence until a full pardon is granted as promised. The deal Allen made was not fulfilled, it was actually a ploy to get him back in camp and let him rot. He will be in prison camp for a long time. As reality sets in for Allen it drives him over the edge. He makes a second escape, and is free from prison camp, but he is a changed man once again. His second escape leaves him broken, and ever fearful that he is about to be caught again and sent back. Allen's life is doomed to be a dark and dangerous one. The film is very well done, and the desperation and feelings of compassion for the main character are palpable. A very gritty, dark, well told story.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>JRamos3</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A stark look at a "real story"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Where to begin? First and foremost, as a veteran this film struck a major cord with me on a personal level. I could relate to Muni's character, James Allen, and his quest to become his own man and not just do what he was expected to because others told him that is what he should do. That brings me to my first point, and a what I believe is a serious theme, or message of the film: self-determination. The inherent right of every person to decide their own fate and path. All James Allen wanted to do was live his life and be the best he could, and Muni's stark portrayal of the character was able to capture this both accurately, and with a chilling connection to the audience. It seems as though everyone else had a plan for James, and all the while, he had his own plans.Clearly from the outset things went awry, and then he found himself on the chaingang; but even the malicious treatment at the hands of the guards, and extremely poor food and housing could not deter his spirit. This is when the film really got good for me. The fact that even after his stint on the chaingang, James was able to successfully and productively rejoin society speaks volumes to the caliber of man he is. That is what I believe is also an extremely endearing message of the film, the unwillingness to accept defeat even in the face of so much adversity. To be sure, the very well done time lapse sequence in the film during which James rises in the hierarchy of "Tri-State Construction" showcases this message. To the casual viewer, this might seem to be just a visually stylish way to pass a long amount of time, however, to someone looking to glean a deeper message from the film, that is what I took away from it.Lastly, but in no way a minor issue, this film brought to light the rather deplorable conditions of the widely used chaingang system that many southern states used to employ. As most would know, this film is based on a true story, and although the name of the state where James is incarcerated is never revealed, some scholarly research led me to find out that the state was Georgia. Clearly, this film does an excellent job utilizing the relatively new technology of sound, it only having been around for about five or six years at the time, and nowhere is there more evident than during the scenes where James is on the chaingang. The scene where James and another prisoner are being whipped for "insubordination" makes excellent use of the contrast of sound, while at the same putting on full display the harsh conditions of the chaingang system. additionally, when the black prisoners begin to engage in singing old spirituals, the viewer, or at least this viewer, is immediately, for lack of a better word, "moved." It is hard to describe the exact feeling, sufficed to say, that the scene is very captivating. Overall, I enjoyed watching this film and would recommend it to others who might be interested in learning about what the world was like back in that time. Oh, and Paul Muni's acting was beyond phenomenal.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>j-catanzaro</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Classic Movie- Must see!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I found this movie to be absolutely incredible in every way given the time period during which it came out. From what I understand this was one of the first full-length "talkies" where actor's spoke and the sound portion was attached directly onto the film as to synchronize the visual and audio together. Although it was black and white, the editors did a great job of splashing hue's of colors onto certain scenes to suggest different times of day. For example, when Allen is in the midst of his 2nd stint with the chain gang the landscape is tinted a brownish-yellow as to suggest the hot sunlight beating down upon the laborers. Scenes occurring at night are shaded with dark blues, gray, and black, and so on.I believe the most surprising part of the whole film was the emotional connection the film was able to make with its viewers. Most old films I have seen are a bit dry in terms of feeling real or making any sort of connection with me as a viewer. Paul Muni does an excellent job of selling his emotions and making us really feel for his character- this was the first pre-1960's movie I've seen that has really been able to establish that connection (and this was made in 1932!).Funny, Dramatic, Exciting, Suspenseful, Action filled, Emotional. Even a car chase to boot! loved this movie, you will too!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I am A Fugitive from a Chain Gang is a very socially conscious film. In a time when many people didn't see films as a means to push a social and political agenda, Mervyn LeRoy's film takes aim at the injustices of the prison system in America. Paul Muni turns in a great performance as James Allen who's wrongful conviction lands him in a southern chain gang. After escaping he is exploited again by the justice system in order for them to save face. On top of pushing a socially and politically charged message, the film is shot incredibly well. The lighting in various scenes captures the mood and feelings of the prisoners as well as portraying the solitude of prison life. The ending of the film contains a great fade out of the scene which has been said to have happened by a lighting accident. IN short, LeRoy was able to combine high artistic quality with a social/political agenda to bring together a fantastic film.</fullReview>

		<username>Seamus Dowling</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A powerful and timeless social message...with a dump truck chase</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mervyn LeRoy's I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang(1932) is a terrific motion picture that has two glaring faults. One, the title is way too damn long, but that had zero impact on my enjoyment of the film. The second and much more meaningful fault is the climactic dump truck escape at the end of the film. This seems like a case where the filmmakers were attempting to throw in some gratuitous and fantastical action into a piece about morality and righteousness as they didn't trust that audiences would pay to watch a film based just on social commentary. Perhaps the filmmakers were correct in that estimation but without the chase scene the film would have been a solid 9 for me, with the chase, it's a 7.The social messages of the film, the mistreatment of war veterans and the cruel and unusual punishment of the chain gang penal system are incredibly powerful. Seeing Paul Muni's heroic and well respected NCO treated so poorly by his family, his former boss and "his girl" upon his return from war and his struggle to re-enter civilian life provides a message that is still incredibly relevant today. The other message, that of the cruel practice of chain gang labor for prisoners, is also still relevant today as even though the practice is a thing of the past, the US continues to struggle with creating a penal system that actually rehabilitates criminals rather than just punishing them. Muni's performance as a strong but broken man is brilliantly complex and subtle. The story paints the character in a bit too positive a light, making him a complete innocent who was just in the wrong place at the wrong time but this was probably done as the filmmakers assumed the audience would have less sympathy for the character if he was an active participant in the initial robbery. But where the story makes Allen a flat good guy, Muni's performance reveals a more interesting dark side to the character.The final chase scene detracts from an otherwise great film that is definitely worth watching for the powerful message and equally powerful performance by Muni.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Sound techniques hit the nail on the head</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What I found particularly interesting in this film was the sophisticated use of sound and light alongside the acting and dialogue. Throughout the film sound was used to evoke imagery, where before in silent films expressions had to create imagery, films could now incorporate sound with movement to emphasise a change and create a connection with the audience. What really resonated with me was using sound to represent time passing. Like when they used the sound of work, whether from the "chain gang" or when the main character, James Allen, is working on the bridge, to make a calendar change from month to month as time passes.I also found the use of light to be equally stimulating. For example, I personally found it intriguing how the time of day affected how easy it was to detect the striped outfit of the "chain gang" members. When it was dark out, they all blended together much like a zebra herd would, but when it became light, you could individually separate them. Whether executed like this on purpose, I found a message in this stating how members of the "chain gang" are viewed in different situations.This film had an especially loud voice since it was based on a true story and uncovered some of the truths of our justice system at the time making it an irreplaceable part of our history.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Paul Muni acts as the devastatingly moving character, James Allen, who returns to his hometown a WWI war hero. Influenced by the work that he did while he was over seas, James, controversially, decides to give up his stable factory job and hit the road in order to find engineering work. Tough travel results in little reward because of the lack of employment opportunities. His true downfall begins when he meets a seemingly kind stranger who holds him at gunpoint while directing him to rob a food joint.James was wrongfully sentenced to 9 years in prison; from there the film begins to exploit the tragic living conditions for prisoners in these "chain-gangs". It was a bold move on Warner Bros. part considering the serious social statement and agenda-setter attitude of the film.Paul Muni managed to capture my heart as his character withstands the turbulent ups and down of then escaping prison, building his reputation as a very well respected engineer - while being blackmailed by his wife - and then having it all ripped away when she finally betrays him after years of marriage. Then he ends up back in jail. Throughout the movie we can feel the injustice through his effectively melodramatic performance.In my day and age is it difficult for young adults to understand this true injustice because of the severe ramifications that have been made to the prison system since the 30s; although it is absolutely correct that this film has withstood the test of time because it questions what our country stands for, Freedom, today we just ask the question in a different language.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Excellent Use of Sound and Lighting</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this wonderful film for my American Cinema class, and was a bit hesitant to let myself like this movie. I am not usually one for a crime drama, let alone a black and white movie. However the fast paced plot as well as Paul Muni's wonderful and believable acting kept me well intrigued. My favorite part about this movie was not the acting or the plot, but the use of sound. In a less than technological age, the use of sound in this movie was spectacular. Sound allowed the audience become aware of their surrounds, allowing for a new dimension of film. When James came home in the very beginning of the film, we heard the boats in the background and when he was on the chain gang, we heard rustle of chains and and hammering of rocks. Sound allowed us as the audience to suffer the pain the man was facing when he was facing a beating in the other room, with the sounds of whipping and yelling. Sound also allowed us to experience the passage of time when the effects of something as the train or the hammering of rocks would fade in out. Amazing movie, amazing acting, amazing effects.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>James Allen (Paul Muni) returns from the military, hoping to start a new life. After venturing out, he gets unknowingly involved in a robbery and sentenced to hard labor. I like the use of maps to show where Allen travels across the country. He may not be getting the construction employment he wanted, at least not at first, but he sure is seeing the country.There is a nice subplot of blackmail, a curious question of how the post office finds people -- or of people opening others' mail -- and a fair amount of twists and turns, perhaps even depth. By 1930s standards, this film is quite rich and should be seen by more people, especially if they like "O Brother, Where Art Thou".</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Beautiful yet dark.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is film is an honest look at the treatment of criminals in a southern prison system after World War I. It depicts the effects of the Great Depression on the common man. The basic theme is that every person deserves their basic civil rights despite their background and that anyone can come from nothing to become a prominent figure in society. The lighting of this film is remarkable. Many scenes are dramatically bathed in darkness letting the viewer see only what is important. Sound also played a big role in setting the film, the constant sound of sledge hammers reoccurs to emphasis the constant struggle of the prisoners. The film also makes clever use of off screen action like in the scene where James is taken to another room to be flogged, the camera does not follow him into the room but remains stationary allowing the viewer to hear the hits of the whip and the cries of pain.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Gritty prison social drama</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So begins the gritty prison drama, or at least an early incarnation of the genre. Paul Muni stars as James Allen, a young man freshly back from WWI who wants to make a name for himself. Instead of returning to his job at a factory, he hits the road looking for an engineering job. He soon arrives in New Orleans, where he is falsely accused of stealing $5 and change. He is sentenced to work on a chain gang. The inhuman conditions eventually drive him to make a daring (and painful) escape. He moves to Chicago where he finally gets a job as an engineer. He works hard for several years, continually being promoted. He ends up being one of the most respected members of Chicago society. Along the way he meets a girl who discovers his past. She uses this blackmail him. He finally calls her bluff when he wants to marry the girl he loves. The blackmailer turns him in to the police, who want to send him back to the chain gang. The city of Chicago does not want him to go. They feel he had paid his debt to society. The prosecutors from Louisiana promise him that if he willingly returns, he will be pardoned after 90 days. He agrees, as he merely wants to move on with his life. Once back on the chain gang, the prison system soon recants on their promise. He escapes once again, this time doomed to spend the rest of his life on the run.It is imminently clear that this film has a social message. The story is based on the true memoirs of Robert E. Burns. He had hoped to expose the cruelty of the chain gangs. One scene in particular that is highly effective is where Allen his plotting his escape. The prisoners are working on removing railroad tracks. He asks a fellow prisoner to hit the shackles on his ankles so that they will bend. He will then be able to slip out of them. This means that his ankle is being hit with a sledge hammer. His desire to be free and the escape the torment of the guards is so strong that he is willing to endure this. In the end it is implied that the injustice of the system has turned him into a fearful, desperate criminal. He is willing to do almost anything to stay alive and free.One particular movie that is of the same vein is The Shawshank Redemption. Both are about a man wrongly accused. Both are about a corrupt system. Both involve escapes. Shawshank is much more about the people and their friendships. IAAFFACG is more socially aware. It is effective at making us empathize with the convict. Paul Muni does an effective job in his role. He has almost a boyish quality about him. In this movie it makes him seem like an innocent everyman. Muni was also in Howard Hawks' Scarface. In that film, Muni's boyish quality makes seem a childish truant, which is what his character essentially is?a heartless, sadistic one at that.LeRoy's direction is very good. His visual style is not overly complicated, but it is nicely creative at times, and far from mundane. There is a scene where footage of the chain gang working is overlapped with the picture of a calender. The men are singing and swinging their hammers in time with the music. The pages of the calender turn with each strike of the hammers. I thought this was an interesting touch. The final scene is also very memorable, as James Allen gradually faces into darkness. Very effective.</fullReview>

		<username>sme_no_densetsu</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Brutal realism stifled by an improbable (but true) story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang", released in 1932, was meant as an indictment of the inhuman conditions faced on chain gangs of the era. While the intent is admirable the execution strikes me as somewhat dishonest.Although the screenplay appears to be pretty close to the truth (at least Robert E. Burns' version of the truth) the character of James Allen seems precisely designed to evoke our sympathies. Of course we'd feel sorry for an innocent man but how many of those on chain gangs were actually innocent? Personally, I'd be more sympathetic if Allen had been driven to commit the crime due to the desperation of his situation. That would give more weight to the secondary theme of the mistreatment of returning WWI vets.As for the acting, Paul Muni gave a good performance in the lead role and was rewarded with an Oscar nomination for his efforts. The supporting cast is uniformly competent but not particularly notable. The direction by Mervyn LeRoy is capable and shows some verve at times. Overall, the film is technically sound if not especially impressive.In the end, the truth upon which the film is based resembles Hollywood fabrication so much that it hurts the picture, especially since realism is one of the film's goals. So, while the film may not be truly dishonest the message is adversely affected by selective honesty.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Steffi_P</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>"You're among friends"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>On the whole, "message" pictures don't tend to do that well. Audiences go to the movies to be entertained, not preached to. But there are exceptions. I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang works because it has the "It could happen to you" factor. It brought the issue home to audiences in a way that they could relate to, and such is its power in immersing us in the plight of its protagonist it still has an impact today.In many ways, Robert E. Burns's autobiographical book on which I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang is based seems tailor-made for cinematic adaptation. When I first saw the movie, I assumed it had been heavily story-fied to add excitement and make the character more sympathetic. However it is more a case of exaggeration than total misrepresentation. Burns was not completely innocent of theft but it's true he was coerced into it. His first escape was more or less exactly as it is represented on screen, although his second was a little less spectacular. Other than that I understand the only changes are to names and other inconsequential details.But it's not so much the fact that this is a true story, it is the way we are drawn into living it. Paul Muni is the perfect player for the part. He basically looks like an average Joe, innocent and good-hearted, but is just about rugged enough to believably get involved in some kind of trouble. More importantly however he is an excellent naturalistic performer, not trying to romanticise the situation but simply reacting like an ordinary feller under pressure. He is absolutely believable in that midpoint of someone who is not quite hardened enough to simply accept his fate, but is plucky enough to make an escape ? as most of us in the audience would probably believe ourselves to be. Interestingly it was around this time that realistic acting was starting to go head-to-head with theatrical hamming. In the end it was an example of the latter ? Charles Laughton as Henry VIII ? that won the Oscar, but this is fair enough. Laughton's is perhaps the most captivating ham act ever committing to celluloid, whereas Muni is merely very good at what he does.The director of I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang was Mervyn LeRoy, a largely forgotten man these days, but at the forefront of the industry in his own time. LeRoy was skilled at giving his pictures real bite and pace. The effect he goes for here is to really involve us in the hero's story. In the first scenes on the chain gang he directs the action so that Muni is out of synch with everything that is going on around him. Muni is effectively us, a stranger in a strange place. After opening a scene with some attention-grabbing close-up, LeRoy often has the camera follow Muni closely, sometimes using point-of-view shots to make our experience match his. In the later scenes, LeRoy makes more use of grim reaction shots and stylised compositions, replacing realism with nightmarish oddity.But what is perhaps the biggest key to I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang's powerful punch is its emphasis on danger and tension. In Muni's cross-country flight during his first escape, we cut between frantic close-ups of Muni and the pack of dogs, too fleeting and indistinct to tell the distance between the two. This was in the days before incidental scores were commonplace, and in this case it's a good thing. How much more exhilarating this scene is with the barking of the dogs as the only backdrop to the frenzy. Paul Muni is an everyman who becomes the wrong man, back when Master of Suspense? Alfred Hitchcock was still in England making half-arsed melodramas. The formulae that Hitch would one day make his own are at work here, in one of the boldest and most haunting pictures of the pre-code era.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Great, Great Movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This movie is a real treat. The plot is riveting and the themes are very relatable. The main character, Allen, is such a dynamic character that the audience is invested in him from the get-go. The film starts off with Allen, fresh out of the army, having to deal with his parents, who have a different vision for his life than he does. This is certainly a relatable problem. Allen rejects their vision and takes hold of his own life saying, "I don't want to be a soldier of anything!" Surely he didn't anticipate the twists and turns his life would take in the following years.This film is interesting because it makes the audience sympathize with Allen and the convicted criminals in the chain gain. We see how Allen is wrongly convicted of a crime. He was truly in the wrong place at the wrong time. The movie makes an enemy out of authority figures such as the police. Furthermore, it makes you wonder how many innocent people are really sent away due to the injustice in our justice system. When Allen escapes from the chain gain, he becomes a valued member of society only to be sent back through another act of deception on part of the justice system, a system that is supposed to be honorable.Cinematically, this movie is truly enjoyable. A strong point of the movie, I thought, was the clever use of transitions. Shots of a moving map compliments with sounds of a boat horn and a train whistle are a creative way to establish the setting and Allen's movements throughout the country as both a vagabond in the beginning and a fugitive in the end. These map shots are also coupled with another layer of film, creating a very cool, translucent shot. Shots of his marriage license and newspaper articles showing that he is on the rise in society are also good transitions. The camera work when he initially escapes creates a really riveting scene. Quick shots of him running through tree branches and of him looking back to the dogs chasing him really make the audience sit at the edge of their seats. The truly amazing part of this movie is the underwater shots when he submerges to avoid detection and capture by the dogs. You see him sitting there as the guard wades closer and closer to where he is. This scene is complimented by the use of subjective sound from Allen's point of view. Above the water the dogs are noisily barking and the guards are shouting. But when the camera is submerged?silence. The movie's ending is also clever. It ends in darkness, showing that Allen will spend the rest of his life on the run, forced to slink in the shadows.This movie is beautifully done and is complimented by great acting, suspense, and unpredictability. The film is fast-paced so the audience is never bored. And when the movie is over, you are left looking at our government and the (in)justice system in a whole new light.</fullReview>

		<username>jhill8</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>"They're the ones who should be in chains!"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" is a 1932 pre-Hay's Code crime drama and an arresting denouncement of the American penal system. The film explores themes of freedom and justice by way of a man who is constantly imprisoned by a society which claims to be "free." Questioning the roles of morality in the world while never coming off as preachy, "Fugitive" works for two reasons: the screenplay, which uses an interesting symmetrical structure and is rife with symbolism, numerology, and amusing one-liners and double entendres, and actor Paul Muni, who bleeds emotion in every scene.The film utilizes some classic "bad to worse" storytelling devices which have made works from the Book of Job to "The Big Lebowski" so entertaining. Namely, a series of wrong place, wrong time coincidences, a corrupt and apathetic authority, and a femme fatale with a hidden agenda. Never becoming melodramatic, the film contains plenty of clever, lighthearted dialogue to ease the mood.The cinematography and lighting is striking throughout, especially during the final scenes. The post-production sound design is ambitious while still amateurish and experimental (screeching tires on a dirt road?). Other than those notable exceptions, the film is solid from a technical standpoint.Muni's portrayal of wrongfully accused convict James Allen is the strongest aspect of the film. Muni turns in an honest, heartfelt performance which slowly crushes the audience as the character himself is crushed by The Powers That Be.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" had such a high level of suspense, frustration and stomach-turning anxiety that I stayed perched at the edge of my chair throughout the entirety of the film. This movie was an enormous success and extremely advanced for its time, not only in its skillful use of sound and camera work but also in its purpose and content. It is clear that the intent of this film was to educate and inform the public of the corrupt brutality of the government and its prison systems. At the time this was a very bold and maybe even risky subject to speak out against and in result caused much controversy. Although much has changed and improved since then, It would be naive of me to not recognize the many flaws, heart-breaking injustices and corrupt ways of our prison systems that still exist to this day. And it is due to this recognition that I believe this movie to be timeless. "I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" has certainly become one of my top five movies from its era thanks to Paul Muni's flawless performance, its passionate political purpose and the over all high quality of the film.</fullReview>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>arts 272-500</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" follows the life of a man, James Allen, who is struggling for control. It seems that his life is not governed by his own choices, but those that are forced upon him. He is an army veteran, returning from basing his day-to-day life off orders and returns to living in his mother's house with dreams of change and finding a job that has importance to him. His mother finds him a clerical job at the factory where he used to work and she tries to force him into it. James spends his days working at the factory with his mind wandering to the construction site he can see through the window and goes there during his lunch breaks. He finally leaves to pursue a life working in construction and hops from town to town looking for work. He finds himself at the mercy of fate when he is offered dinner by a new friend. At the diner, he is held at gunpoint to steal money from the cash register and runs away during the confusion when cops come in. Since he was caught running away with the money on him, he is found guilty even though he was forced to steal. James is sentenced to ten years of labor in a chain gang and successfully escapes after a year. He finds a new job under the name "Allen James" and through hard work becomes very influential and wealthy. His landlord Marie reads a letter from his brother and learns that he is a fugitive. She blackmails him into marriage so she can live off of his money. He falls in love with another woman Helen and wants to divorce his wife. His wife betrays him and spills his secret and he is approached by officials to negotiate a pardon. James fulfills his requirements for his pardon but is retained to finish his previous sentence. He escapes again and approaches his lover Helen. Before he leaves her, she asks how he will live without money and as he disappears into the dark he replies "I steal". These last words have a great significance to the theme of the movie. He is ultimately forced to steal because he was wrongly accused of stealing. The misfortune he encounters takes a good, hardworking man and reduces him into exactly the thing that society was trying to prevent: a criminal.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A Must See Film!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The American Justice System, with all its rehab,community service, and prison systems, have made a huge progression towards improvement throughout the past century. Prison systems today, treat their prisoners fairly and help prisoners move their way back to a normal, crime-free life. By serving time, prisoners learn that committing crime is a bad thing that they can't escape from in life. The prison system, from the late 19th-20th century, was horrible. Prisoners were often put into prison for crimes that they weren't committed for while other prisoners were put into jail for the usual crimes of theft and murder. The prisoners were given unhealthy food to eat, had to wear shackles on their legs all the time, and washed their arms and face in a single bucket. When the prisoners misbehaved, their officer would push them to the ground or whip their backs with a belt buckle until they bled.Burns has shown the hardships of prisoners in this film well and to the point. The background music in a few scenes was filled with recorded music full of train whistles, moving machinery, bombing sounds, and barking dogs. The actor's performances throughout the movie were believable and made you wonder what was going to happen to the characters. The cinematography from scene to scene was really neat as the cameras double exposed pictures, letters and numbers to show how much time had elapsed in various scenes.I would recommend this movie to anybody that is interested in learning about the early functioning of the prison system and is interested in being immersed in a great storyline that will keep you wondering what is going to happen to the characters in the movie.</fullReview>

		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Intense movie and that's not just considering the time.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In a time when talkies were new and needed to prove how much sound was necessary, a movie came along and showed how great directing, editing, and acting could come together. This movie wasn't the only movie or the first movie to prove that sound wasn't just another novelty, but it used it to tell a story very well without revealing too much to sensitive viewers. One scene which involves inmates being flogged as punishment doesn't show anything more than the shadows of the people involved and the sounds of the beatings. The scene came off as very dark and showed just how dark the rest of the movie was going to be. Other uses of sound and lighting made the movie more and more immersing. Another good use of sound for story telling was when the main character tried to hide in a creek and it switched between shots of him which were dead silent and shots of the guards looking for him. Moments like this were very suspenseful. It can be easy to forget how old this movie is.Except for the occasionally optimistic scenes, it is either suspenseful or depressing or both. It is not a movie to watch to feel better. It is a movie to make people think which was innovative for 1932. It was one of the first movies to be this political. Paul Muni's performance as James Allen is extraordinary. It reminds me of Humphrey Bogart, another great actor of the time who stole the show in any movie. He makes you believe in his character and understand everything that is going on with him. One interesting theme in the movie is James' constant need to make a choice about what is a worse way to live. As an inmate slave or in a life that suppresses him just as much? This movie also doesn't forget its silent roots. Like silent movies that have little blurbs narrating a scene, this movie has that but in the form of a paycheck or a newspaper headline to get the viewer more into the story and world. If you want a good political movie brings out many emotions in you, then this is for you. Ignore the year it came out. It's about as exciting as some of today's movies. It also has a very interesting conclusion that will leave most thinking about the movie for a while. I wish I could talk about it more without ruining it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>I Am A Fugitive from a Chain Gang, directed by Mervyn Leroy is a film that was far ahead of its time. Being one of the first sound films, director Mervyn Leroy wasted no time utilizing the new sound technology. Numerous scenes stand out with excessively loud sounds and noise that help depict the feeling of the characters in certain situations. The chain gang is given its character through the sounds that are produced by chains, pickaxes, and blood hounds. The sounds in these scenes create a particular mood that makes one feel and understand the brutality of the chain gang. It is interesting to see how and where Leroy used sound excessively throughout the movie, the only scenes where the sound stands out is in the chain gang scenes. Perhaps this is Leroy's way of drawing the audience in during the important scenes, it certainly makes the brutality of the chain gang much more real and present for those watching the film. Throughout the film there are some very interesting small details that a presented in the script and cinematography. For one, Leroy uses cinematic foreshadowing throughout the film, one of the most noticeable is the driving scene in the start and the final chase scene of the movie. Early in the movie Allen is driving a logging truck through treacherous steep hills while on the job somewhere in the country, this scene seems to portray some sort of darkness and doom but at this early stage in the film you wouldn't know what Allen's future held. The end chase scene is shot exactly the same, still creating some sort of feeling of impending doom. Perhaps Leroy didn't plan this minor foreshadow, but both scenes paint a picture of Allen running from something, trying to make it towards the future. I Am A Fugitive From a Chain Gang is a must see for any movie watcher, Leroy creates an astonishingly modern feel with his cinematography and sound. If you want to see good acting and a developed plot, check out this film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Paul Muni Steals Away Into The Night</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Following service in World War I, decorated soldier Paul Muni (as James Allen) wants to pursue a job in engineering, rather than keep to the monotonous factory job that was held open for him. While struggling to find a job in construction, Mr. Muni meets similarly downtrodden Preston Foster (as Pete). The men go to a diner where Muni is promised a free meal; instead, Muni becomes an unwitting robbery accomplice. After a shooting, Muni is arrested, convicted, and sentenced to work on a chain gang. The title, "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang", is an accurate description of what comes next...Another great performance from Muni, who was also seen, during the year, in "Scarface" (on screen) and "Counselor-at-Law" (on stage). Returning to films in 1932, Muni clearly established himself as one of the best and most respected professional actors in the business; he reached #2 in the "Academy Awards" voting for "Best Actor" of the year (behind Charles Laughton in "The Private Life of Henry VIII"). His "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" deservedly won the "National Board of Review" award for "Best Picture" and placed on most film organizations' "Bests" lists.Director Mervyn LeRoy, photographer Sol Polito, and the writers were also award-worthy. The story is based on (more or less) Robert Elliott Burns' autobiographical account of his experiences on an actual Georgia chain gang. It brought about changes in, and attitudes about, prison "correction" facilities, which were obviously encouraging individuals to become more hardened criminals. The supporting performances are a mixed bag, with Glenda Farrell (Marie) and Noel Francis (Linda) standing out, as two women unabashedly eager for sexual relations with Muni... Definitely recommended.********* I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang (11/19/32) Mervyn LeRoy ~ Paul Muni, Helen Vinson, Glenda Farrell, Noel Francis</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>"I'll be a model prisoner if it kills me".</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on the real life true story of Robert E. Burns, Warner Brothers offers yet another in a long line of social conscience films that made them a studio juggernaut as early as the 1930's. Unlike Cagney, Bogart or Edward G. Robinson however, this picture's star Paul Muni isn't remembered today except by true film enthusiasts. In his time though, Muni was such a prolific actor that he became a victim of his own success. The studio began billing him as MR. Paul Muni, thereby alienating the common man audience that built him up in the first place. In "I Am a Fugitive From a Chain Gang", Muni is front and center throughout virtually the entire movie, and as the story progresses, it's impossible to take your eyes off him.What makes the central character's story truly horrific begins with his sentence - ten years of hard labor based on circumstantial evidence pointing to a robbery of five dollars. In a split second, James Allen's (Muni) life is derailed and sent into a tailspin of unintended consequences. His life on a chain gang documents such indignities as having to request permission to wipe the sweat off his face, or repeated lashes from the duty guard's whip for making a snide remark. It's no wonder that Allen is driven to 'hang it on a limb', insider code for bustin' out. The irony of Allen's breakout is not so much that he's eventually tracked down by the authorities, but that he's subjected to another round of inhumane prison treatment after he turns himself in as part of a phony deal with the state in which he served time (Georgia, though it was never mentioned by name in the picture).Aside from the gripping story, I'm always intrigued by films of the Thirties and Forties that reveal glimpses of life from so long ago. James Allen's cross country jaunts while looking for work reveal that a flophouse bed and meal go for fifteen cents each; throw in another nickel for a bath. As for wages, Allen took his first paying laborer job at four dollars a day, and was living pretty well by the time he made the grade as an assistant superintendent at fourteen dollars a day! But the real sit up and take notice scene for me was during the conversation Allen had with new found girlfriend Helen after leaving the dinner party - she declared she could do anything she wanted being 'free, white and twenty one'! I'll agree with most viewers who hold that the picture's ending is one of the most powerful in cinema, a haunting image and line uttered - "I steal' - to cement the sense of desperation in the main character's doomed future. Watching the picture tonight on Turner Classics, host Robert Osborne mentioned that Paul Muni's dramatic fade into darkness was one of those most fortunate accidents in film history in which a light blew out precisely as the scene ended. It was almost re-shot too, until someone said wait a minute. I just love those throw away bits of film trivia.</fullReview>

		<username>SnorrSm1989</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Challenging the effects of prison life in the 1920s; thought-provoking and also quite entertaining</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I discovered that this film, starring Paul Muni was airing on TCM one night, I could not resist watching it despite being quite exhausted that day. Well, so much for that; after ten minutes I was wide awake, completely captivated by the performances, direction, dialogue, and, most notably, the treatment of a subject which remains as relevant today as back then: the effects of prison life.Set during the 1920s, I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG covers the struggles of honest and kind young man James Allen (Paul Muni) as he is wrongly accused of a robbery. Allen soon finds himself sweating as a member of a chain gang in prison, carving stone and experiencing brutal treatment from fellow prisoners and officers alike. He eventually manages to escape to Chicago, where he slowly works his way up to success and becomes a highly respected construction engineer. However, while things finally seem to get going for him, the prison has not forgotten his escape? I won't reveal anything further. The movie speaks for itself. Being released in 1932, this haunting depiction of a brutal life inside an American prison has scarcely aged a bit. True enough, American prisons have undergone changes during the seventy-five years that have passed, few of them forcing prisoners to carve stone anymore, but CHAIN GANG explores a question which is still too often ignored: what should be considered the precise purpose of prisons? Does the legal system live up to its title and reputation? The movie appears to be quite critical as to how prisons are devised, Muni's character serving as an example of one man who really wasn't very bad before being tied to the chains, only to be changed for the worse once that happened. At the same time, it should be noted that the movie does not appear to carry any agenda, as most of the prisoners are actually characterized rather unflatteringly. Then again, a next question would be, of course, if not many of the least flattering aspects of the prisoners become even more pronounced after they've entered prison life.All this being said, if you believe the legal system to be excellent, or even if you don't care for the topic at all, CHAIN GANG offers plenty to enjoy in any case. The persons involved in the story undergo strong character development; there are several action-packed sequences included; and even quite a few hints of sex (after all, the movie was fortunate enough to be a so-called Pre-Code). Hats off to director Mervyn LeRoy, who did his possibly best achievement throughout his career; he took great advantage of camera- setups and "fade-outs" which were technically quite impressive for the time and added further to the emotional impact of the film. Also worthy of a special mention is Robert Burns, author of the book on which the movie is based, and Howard Green and Brown Holmes, who did the screen-play and are the brains behind some of the memorable dialogue. Yet, the man whose contribution remains the most outstanding is the lead player himself, Paul Muni. Muni was one of the very first movie stars who did not cling to one particular "image" or way of presenting himself in every movie he was in. Instead, he actually tried to understand the particular mental state and situations of each character he played, developing his own personality along with his character; hence his performances stand out as captivating now as back then. It is hard to compare the gentle Allen we see at first with the Allen of later on in the movie, as the prison arguably makes a criminal out of him.I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG did quite well at the box-office when originally released and was nominated for no less than three Academy Awards, which, I'd say, confirms that America was open for some criticism of the legal system even in those years. Oddly, the film has by now become quite obscure and is, like Muni, seldom given a mention even among buffs of "the classics." Showings on TCM and a release on DVD may help to increase its popularity, which is not a day too soon.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>When we view a Drama, a Good Drama that is, we can get so wrapped up in the story that we forget that the story in front of us is just that-a STORY! As a work of fiction, even a good, well written and well made movie is still make believe. As such a fictional storyline is subject to lean toward the beliefs of the Writer, the Director and whoever is involved in the production. Even the Actors can cause a story's political content; but it's definite that Writers and Directors who have the most influence.And in not being unfair to the Author, Mr. Robert E. Burns, whose autobiography was the basis for the screenplay. The story is brutal, unglamorized and put together in the best tradition of the early talkies.But we still must approach this and any fiction before us simply because of the irresistible trend of Hollywood to show us the story of the wrongly accused, the guy who is caught in the wrong place at the wrong time. Hence we can offer examples of this plot gimmick.Next to our film to be examined, I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG (1932); we could cite Alfred Hitchcock's NORTH BY NORTHWEST (1959). We could, so we will. In it, Cary Grant's character is victimized of not only being the killer, but also is a victim of one big case of Internal Mistaken Identity.And from 1963 to '67, we all sat by our TV sets watching Actor Barry Morse as the Guy from the Indianapolis Police Department wit the super-big Expense Account, Lieutenant Gerard as he dutifully pursued Dr. Richard Kimble (David Jansen) from one end of the next corner. Once again we have a case of another innocent man's being falsely accused Mel Brooks even spoofed the situational premise in his Hitchcock spoof, HIGH ANXIETY (1977)..As for our CHAIN GANG Movie, it was obviously painstakingly constructed and was given a firm, sound foundation. In a story such as this, you can't just get to the incarcerated road crew without a big build-up first.So our story begins with a view of a multitude of American Soldiers, called "Doughboys", aboard a transport ship returning from World War I, from that "War to end all Wars." We meet up with our main character, one James Allen (Paul Muni) who expresses his desire to become a construction engineer; utilizing his experience in the Army Corps of Engineers.Once home it seems that everyone is out to map out his future; so he sets out to find his entrance level job in construction as a start. Jobs being scarce, he starts an Odyssey across the Eastern part of the country, traveling by any means; be it Steamship, Passenger Railway or hopping freights. He is reduced to the status of a Hobo, but still he persists and winds up in the middle of a robbery, which go him into his being sentenced to the Chain Gang in the first place.Aside from the story of the falsely accused and his dealings with a brutal, overbearing penal system, we are given a closely crafted look at the conditions of poverty that existed, especially in some rural areas. And remember, this was before the Great Depression.</fullReview>

		<username>Sean Couch (sean.couch@ttu.edu)</username>

		<shortDescription>A Instant Classic In My Book</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I Am A Fugitive From A Chain Gang" is a instant classic. It was directed by Mervyn LeRoy and released to the public in 1932 and yet you can still find similarities between our generation of films we watch on the silver screen today, making it a timeless masterpiece that was nominated for three Oscars. The film incorporates a thrilling plot telling the story of a young man named James Allen, played by Paul Muni, who was wrongly convicted in the state of Georgia for robbing a diner when he was merely forced to do so by a man he had just met. He is then sent to a prison were brutal tactics are used to help put prisoners through a rigorous correctional program known as chain gangs. After escaping from the "correctional institution" James Allen makes his way to Chicago were he become a prestigious engineer. He is soon caught after being blackmailed and sent back to Georgia were he was promised to serve 90 days and then he shall be pardoned. He soon discovers that he would not be pardoned but would serve the rest of his nine years in one of the toughest chain gang camps there is in Georgia. He escapes for a second time and finds himself losing everything and living as a thief to make ends meet. Ironic that he is put into prison for being wrongfully accused as a criminal and only to later to become a actual criminal. This movie demonstrates the early stages of the film industry that has now become a media giant in the world we live in today. The advancement of dialogue, music, ambient sound and sound effects are clearly present in this film. Clearly we are far more advanced today in our generation of film; but when we look and marvel at the advancement made in just a few years from the early nineteen hundreds to the production of this film, it is clear to see that they had come a far way. The mise en scene, meaning, the transition from scene to scene and the angles in which the film was shot allowed you to be better submerged into the plot. There was great continuity were films in earlier years such as Muybridge and Edison films left you with experimental elements and the study of motion and sound. This continuity is demonstrated when James Allen arrives in prison and throughout the transition we will see a transparent calendar appear that turns pages as we can still see the main character working hard in his daily chores. Slowly you start to see the character transition from what was a clean cut perhaps determined man and is soon rundown by the prison camp and the passing time. Other tools that helped transition from scene to scene was the display of the map. As James Allen makes his way from place to place we see a visual stimulation of a map that presents us his location. This helps because without this assistance the viewer would be left to assume that perhaps he never left his home town.The diegetic sound in any film plays a key role but in the case of this particular production we see a need for more of a clean cut transition from scene to scene. Sounds that played a key role were perhaps the gunshots you hear as James Allen attempts to escape or even more important are the sounds of a bridge being built near by. James Allen had a dream to become a construction engineer and while working at the factory he so desperately detested that was the only sound that brought him comfort and even joy. He had a fascination for building bridges and roads, which later allowed him to become a prestigious man in Chicago before being captured. The sounds as well helped the transition when the director wants us to know that the character is traveling; we hear the train on the tracks or the sound of a boat hitting the waves as it makes it way down the coast line. The non- diegetic sounds do not play a role at all in this film. There is no background music, or theme song that helps derive any emotion from what is going on in the film. This is another difference perhaps between this newer age film then opposed to older films in the nineteen hundreds. There was no dialogue. You would have to read subtitles or script cards while listening to a record player that would be located in the theater and not on the film were it could be linked to go simultaneously with the events taking place during the film.Cinematography has come a long way and will continue to grow through the ages but it is always important to know were we began and still admire the work of those who helped us transition forward in time. This movie presents us with many elements that make it a great film that demonstrates the time while providing great entertainment.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>"That's the sound of the men..."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What a cracking film! Hollywood really hit its stride in the early 30's with musicals, action thrillers and docu - dramas (such as this), amazingly just a few short years after the introduction of talkies, the earliest examples of which were weighed down with stagy or worse, hackneyed silent-movie type acting (all hands and eyes to the heavens, if you get my meaning), prosaic dialogue and set bound locations. I really couldn't believe such a gritty, well acted, fast moving film could emerge in only 1932. Not only does the film take a morally brave stand against the brutal chain - gang regime prevalent particularly in the southern states(although never naming Georgia as the offending state, the frequent interpolation of a U.S. map always just stopping short of Georgia gives a clue as to the intended target), it never lets the message get in the way of good old-fashioned cinematic tension and thrills. There are two great scenes I particularly enjoyed. Firstly, Muni's daring escape from the pursuing prison guards and their baying pack of bloodhounds, climaxing in a brilliant underwater shot of Muni breathing underwater through a hollow tube of vegetation as a guard moves ever closer to where he's lying down and secondly Muni's later encounter with a friendly prostitute after months of degrading incarceration in an all-male confinement - the camera subjectively but not lewdly takes in the woman's form from head to toe, with a tantalising glimpse of stockinged ankle demonstrating subtly, especially in the pre - Hays days, his manly urges, ending with a lovely fade as they inevitably come together and her line "We're all friends here...". There's a little bit of hamminess in some of the playing (I'm thinking of the melodramatic demise of his fellow inmate off the side of their getaway truck and the over the top playing of his shrewish wife) and the insertion of the goody two shoes minister brother jars, but at the film's centre is a powerhouse performance by Paul Muni which engages and inspires in equal measures. The direction is excellent using realistic locations and climaxing in a superb off-beat final scene - no happy ending here. Highly recommended.</fullReview>
