		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Movie Odyssey Review #072: I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>072: I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang (1932) - released 11/19/1932; viewed 7/2/06.The second wife of Josef Stalin, Nadezhda Alliluyeva, is found dead in her home.BIRTHS: Petula Clark.DOUG: In late 1932, Warner Bros. releases a controversial and unforgettable film, based on a true story, exposing the brutal chain gang system of Georgia. Easily one of the best films of that year (along with 'Trouble in Paradise' and 'Scarface'), 'I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang' is an excellent example of the kind of films that the Warner's studio specialized in. I'm really getting a sense of the brand identity of the various studios. Warner Brothers delivered these gritty, controversial, hard-hitting films about relevant topics, mostly gangster films. Paul Muni plays James Allan, a man arrested for petty theft and sentenced to ten years, suffering under a brutal, inhumane chain gang system. He escapes and manages to become a successful businessman in Illinois, but his past soon catches up with him. The two states engage in a tug-of-war, with Illinois wanting to hang on to its illustrious businessman and the southern state (which is never named) wanting to retrieve an escaped fugitive. (Get a load of the Chairman, using Allen's own "rehabilitation," to justify the effectiveness of the chain gang system, but still advocates bringing Allen back to serve his time.) You know it's all true. It's good to see Paul Muni again. His character here is in many ways the opposite of Tony Camonte. James Allan is trying to get by within the law and is cheated by the system into serving time while Tony gets away with all manner of crime while remaining untouched by the law. James tries desperately to be a good man, while Tony delights in being bad. James Allen's situation creates a feeling of "This could happen to you!" During the Great Depression, it was probably a very relevant notion. The movie was controversial enough to be banned in Georgia. I wonder if there was a meeting in which the Georgia representatives told Warner Brothers "We'll release the film if you remove all direct references to the state of Georgia," to which the WB guys would have responded, "Okay. Oops, there aren't any, jerk." By ending the movie the way they do, it not only gives the audience a chill, but also makes it so that the story of James Allen could be the story of anyone in the audience, and it's up to the audience to solve it. The moral of the story? After you escape from prison, you NEVER go back.KEVIN: No film we've viewed before this has showcased the gritty despair of the Great Depression as much as 'I am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang,' a film that ushered in an era of Warner Bros. Studios as a maker of socially conscious films. Before this there was 'The Big House,' but even that film seemed bright and campy compared to this one. I totally buy the harrowing experiences depicted in this film as what prisoners really went through at the time. You're charged with a crime, and you're sent to a place where you are less than human. It doesn't matter who you are or what you did, you are going to pay for all your sins ten times over. This film shows one of the reasons why police officers are tasered before they are issued a taser to use. The people who pass down that sentence simply do not know what it's like for the prisoners because their minds still operate under the just-world phenomenon of "you get what you deserve." If it hurts, you had it coming. They neither know nor want to know what goes on behind those walls. Paul Muni's performance as the titular prisoner is 100% perfect. There is not one moment in the film where we're not feeling what he's feeling. This is one of the most intense movies that we've watched. It's the most riveting kind of social commentary because it ends with a problem. The hero doesn't win when the credits roll. A good man is on the run. What are you going to do about it? It's kind of like stopping in the middle of the story, but it's completely necessary because in reality, the story isn't over. Robert Elliott Burns, the man upon whom the film is based, was still a fugitive when the film was completed, and the film became another part of the story in itself, showing on the silver screen his true suffering at the hands of the justice system.Last film: Boudu Saved from Drowning (1932). Next film: Sign of the Cross (1932).</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Masterful chain gang drama is still powerful stuff...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just as wrenching in its own way as COOL HAND Luke would be, years later, is I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG. Paul Muni gives an unforgettable performance as a tragic man who becomes a victim of circumstance simply by being in the wrong place at the wrong timeThe depression-era drama is said to be based on a true story, but whether it is or not, it is presented in powerful fashion by director Mervyn LeRoy, usually not a director one associates with this kind of gritty material. After leaving Warner Bros., he was responsible for films like LITTLE WOMEN and RANDOM HARVEST at MGM.There is almost no humor throughout the tense running time with the possible exception of the good-humored ALLEN JENKINS as one of the luckier prisoners. Muni's character is punished for a crime he committed at the point of a gun (by PRESTON FOSTER), and finds himself committed to a chain gang for a holdup instigated by Foster.After witnessing the brutal treatment of prisoners, he's determined to escape and he does succeed, even managing to assume a job in the construction business and rising within the ranks to become a productive member of society. But when his whereabouts are disclosed after several years of freedom, by a jealous wife, he decides to make a plea deal that allows him to return to a prison camp for a period of ninety days, after which he'll be automatically released. He goes back but soon learns that the deal is off and the state wants payback for his former escape and an indefinite time of service.Muni pulls another escape and the film ends on a stark note with him retreating into the shadows as the woman he loves pleads with him not to desert her again. The memorable final scene has her saying, "But what will you do? How will you live?" and his reply, with a frantic look on his face, is "I steal." Muni is excellent in the lead and confirms his reputation as the great dramatic actor of the Warner lot with this iconic role. It's a commanding but natural performance, never overstated and the film remains a powerful exposure of injustices in the penal system of the '30s.</fullReview>
	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2006-06-23</date>
		<username>evanston_dad</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Workin' on a Chain Gang</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" was Warners' first major bid at prestige film-making, but it's essentially a gussied up version of the studio's bread and butter, the cheap, gritty gangster films, which were enjoying enormous success at the time "Fugitive" was released.The Warners knew they had a sure-fire box office formula with their gangster films, and "Fugitive" sticks closely to it. There's the same emphasis on the seedy underbelly of mainstream America, where the hobos, prostitutes and criminals call the shots. There's the inexorable struggle of the protagonist against a cold fate; the film records his slow and agonizing slide toward destruction. And there's that instantly recognizable Warners look, ratty and realistic.The first half of the film is strong stuff--the screenplay is taut and economical. There's a harsh indictment not only of the chain gang system, but of post-WWI American life in general. James Allen (Paul Muni) move from one symbolic chain gang to another, whether it's the strict regimentation of the armed services, the mind-numbing sameness of blue collar labor, or the suffocating entrapment of a miserable marriage. He starts out as an innocent, and is made a criminal by a harsh and unforgiving society. Of course, this is 1932, so subtlety is in short supply, and the liberal Hollywood guilt at work stacks the deck shamelessly in Allen's favor. The second half of the film sags and becomes ever so slightly ponderous, but the final image of the film, and that final line, delivered by Muni out of the pitch dark -- "I steal" -- leaves a powerful impression.I wasn't that familiar with Paul Muni before seeing this; he's one of those actors I've heard more about than actually seen. I know he became infamous later in his career as a hopeless over-actor, but he does pretty well here. He's got a tough job, as he's in virtually every frame of the film, and it's only toward the end, as Allen nears the end of his rope, that his histrionics threaten to cushion the impact of his performance. But director Mervyn LeRoy keeps things moving at a decent clip, so there's not a lot of time to dwell on individual scenes.It's astonishing now to think that this film was a tremendous box office success, but it was. How times have changed. I can't imagine a film this downbeat finding an audience today. Maybe the Depression created an atmosphere in which people found comfort in seeing the suffering of others -- the "it could be a lot worse" factor. Or maybe it was just good timing or good marketing.This is a harsh film that has aged well, and retains a surprising ability to shock and enrage, even today. Well done.Grade: A-</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A strong classic that never ages!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>James Allen (Paul Muni) returns from military service after World War I to an America pocked with poor job prospects. Forced to travel state-to-state, seeking work in his trade, the engineering field, Allen winds up in a flop house. He quickly establishes a friendship with the equally desperate Pete (Preston Foster, bursting with a sleazy smile) Pete turns vicious and involves the innocent Allen in a hastily planned diner hold-up. The police kill Pete and capture Allen. The courts believe Allen to be a culprit, and not a victim. Allen is sentenced to ten years hard labor on a chain gang.At the camp, Allen and the other prisoners are forced to endure 15 hour work-days pounding rocks in the hot sun. They are fed greasy, indigestible food, and are subjected to nightly beatings by sadistic wanna-be tough guy guards. In a scene staged and edited well ahead of it's time, Allen escapes.Two very talented men help shape CHAIN GANG into a still riveting classic, actor Paul Muni, a veteran of Second Avenue's Yiddish Theatre, and Mervyn LeRoy, an under-appreciated studio director who knew how to tell a story in a fast, brisk cinematic manner.While Muni in other films (SCARFACE, THE GOOD EARTH) would perform over the top, here he's restrained and calculating. Just watch the way his body jerks and his eyes shifts when he's on the run, fearing the police are right behind him, or in more simpler scenes, the way his body slumps when his minister brother chides him for reaching beyond his means. It was a shame Muni made so few films. This honest, believable actor is surely of the Russell Crowe/Tom Cruise caliber.</fullReview>
	</review>

The tragedy continues with depiction of the cruelty of the guards and various attempts at escape. Once our hero does escape and proves his worth to society with success, he finds that he isn't quite out of the woods yet. Despite the obvious restrictions of film-making at the time, this film is very potent. Some bits are genuinely heart-rending and the ending is one of the saddest and most powerful I've seen. I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG is a movie worth searching for. Like many great 30s dramas, if you give this movie a chance you'll be well rewarded.</fullReview>

I AM A FUGITIVE FROM A CHAIN GANG is a classic later joined by COOL HAND LUKE (1967) in showing the horror of the chain gang system, and later made fun of in SULLIVAN'S TRAVELS (1941). 		<shortDescription>Excellent 'bio pic' from the 30's: worth a rent</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a film by director Mervin Leroy from the fledgling years of sound back in the early 30's. It has been referred to as a "bio-pic". The story line was riveting, so much so that I despised the ending even though it delivered the most powerful and appropriate message for this movie.The movie begins below deck of a states-bound ship from WWI. James Allen, (played by Paul Muni) appeared a bit rushed and unnatural in delivering his lines as did all the characters at first. Time leveled this out and there was a better flow of acting as the film progressed. Muni began to resemble an early "rebel without a cause" character in the dinner scene when he voiced his dissatisfaction with his brother's and mother's plan for his career. It must have seemed very defiant to the audiences of that day as was the provocative dialogue between he and his landlady/first wife. Her come-ons were considered aggressive even by today's standards. I enjoyed the cinematography of this film especially the use of shadow.I also admired the message portrayed by Leroy when he showed James Allen to be a man willing to pursue his dream and passion despite the cost. The cost was the wrongful charge and unfair punishment of hard labor for stealing. The message of irony in the end that demonstrated how the legal system accomplished the exact opposite of what its designed for was brilliant. The court system, hard labor camp, and cold society made a thief out of an innocent man with a dream. This was successful in eliciting emotion in the audience. I identified with Muni's character in that I wanted to see more of his story: how he evaded the police, wrote/published his book, and secured a position as a Hollywood script consultant. The abrupt "I steal!" ending was very meaningful but I left the screening wanting more.</fullReview>

He is wrongly convicted to serve in a brutal chain gang system for ten years. He escapes, moves to Chicago, where he becomes a prominent citizen and superintendent of a construction business. Due to a letter of his stupid brother, Rev. Robert Clinton Allen (Hale Hamilton), his girl-friend Marie Woods (Glenda Farrell) finds his condition of fugitive and blackmails him, forcing James to marry her. He becomes upset with the situation of permanently cheated husband, asks for the divorce and she denounces him to the police. During an intense dispute between the States of Illinois, asking for his pardon, and the Southern State, demanding the return of James to the prison, the brutal chain gang system is exposed, with tragic consequences for James.This movie is an impressive and unforgettable story of social injustice. The saga of James Allen seems to be inspired in the unfair misery of Jean Valjean in Victor Hugo's "Les Misrables"; however, it is incredibly based on the true story of Robert E. Burns (see IMDb Trivia). The sad and heartbreaking conclusion of the story, with James Allen becoming a criminal, is cruel and a severe critic to this medieval penal system. For me, being that I myself have spent time running from the law for reasons that I thought were...well bullshit, I could empathize for James Allen (Muni) through his journey running from the oppressive, and closed minded "man".</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>From director Melvyn LeRoy (Little Caesar, Mister Roberts), this film featured in the book of the 1001 Movies You Must See Before You Die was one that I was looking forward to, because I really like classic crime based films. Basically James Allen (Oscar nominated Paul Muni), a sergeant in the army, has returned home to civilisation having served in Worlf War I, but is restless following his experience, and he cannot get to grips with working a tedious office clerk job, he wants to become an engineer, and his family do not react well to this announcement, they are outraged. He tries to find work after leaving home and he is open to any sort of career where his interest would be used, but only labour jobs without any skill required are available and in many places, he finds it hard to get work he wants to do, and he sinks slowly into poverty. Living in a shelter he is one day asked to come out for a hamburger with his friend, but the friend actually intends to rob the diner they go to, and despite it not having much money his friend places the money in Allen's pocket, and he is caught and wrongly accused of robbery. He is sentenced to ten years in prison and made to work as part of a brutal Southern chain gang, and these chained men are all forced to do manual labour, such as pickaxing rocks and fixing rail tracks, and the prisoners know that there are two options to get out, escape or serve your time with good behaviour. Allen decides when he has the opportunity to escape, and to do this he has another prisoner bash he chains with a sledgehammer to loosen the cuffs, and then he is able to sneak into the woods, take off the shackles, and he runs and escapes. Back in the city of Chicago with some money from one of the other prisoners he buys himself a new attire and has a shave, and then with an address given to him he is able to find a person to let him hide out while the police search for him. Looking for work and giving himself the new name Allen James he slowly builds himself up in the world of construction, until he is much higher up in the business world and becomes successful, but he does have some problems with his boardinghouse proprietor Marie Woods (Glenda Farrell). Having become involved with her she finds out his secret of being an escaped chain gang prisoner, and she blackmails him that if he does not marry her she will tell the authorities, so he agrees to this, and they stay married despite being unhappy with each other. He then finds true love with Helen (Helen Vinson) and wants to marry her, he asks his wife to grant him a divorce, but she keeps him on a leash and refuses, and worse, she betrays him and gets him caught to be sent back to prison. The authorities determine that if Allen hands himself in serves a set time in prison he will be pardoned, having become a respected member of society and creating successful business and construction, Allen accepts and returns to prison. Of course it is when the time he was told he would be released comes that he finds out the pardon was in fact a ruse, the authorities simply wanted him back behind bars and in chains, so again he sees the only choice of freedom is escape. Allen manages to get away again, but he cannot go back into the business world and will be forced to stay on the run, so before he disappears he visits Helen in the darkness and tells her he will be leaving forever, before departing her she asks "How will you live?", and going into the shadow he only replies "I steal." Also starring Noel Francis as Linda, Preston Foster as Pete, Allen Jenkins as Barney Sykes, Berton Churchill as The Judge, Edward Ellis as Bomber Wells, David Landau as The Warden, Hale Hamilton as Rev. Robert Clinton Allen, Sally Blane as Alice, Louise Carter as Mrs. Allen and Willard Robertson as Prison Board Chairman. It was nominated the Oscars for Best Picture and Best Sound, Recording. Very good!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>the inhumanity towards WWI vets</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The title may make the movie sound like a silly comedy, but what "I Am a Fugitive from a Chain Gang" portrays is no laughing matter. Based on the true story of Robert E. Burns, WWI vet James Allen (Paul Muni) is falsely accused of robbery and sentenced to work on a chain gang in Georgia. The horrible conditions on the chain gang are worse than what anyone might have imagined. Fortunately, Allen manages to escape. Of course, then he has to live his life on the lam.Above all, "IAAFFACG" is a staunch indictment of the criminal justice (or rather "injustice") system in the early 20th century. It shows how this man served his country in wartime, and was subsequently treated like dirt. Movies like these should serve as a reminder to us all of how prison life is.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2005-08-12</date>
		<username>silverscreen888</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Powerful and Understated; Fictionalized Biography of Consistent Quality</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a fictionalized biography, "I Am a Fugitive From a Chain Gang" was one of the most influential films made in early Hollywood. It starred character actor Paul Muni as a man who had participated in a small robbery who was hounded because he later escaped from a chain gang--in Georgia, but not identified as such--for years afterward. In fact, he was again a fugitive at the time the film was released. The story line takes up the cautionary tale of Robert E. Burns, here named James Allen, who returns from WWI after doing engineering work; unable to find paying work, he goes with a man because he is hungry to commit a $5 robbery. The other man pulls a gun and shoots at the police who are called; when the other man is killed, he is sentenced to ten years at hard labor. This means being chained within barracks and wearing a striped uniform, being fed swill and breaking up rocks on the roads from dawn until dark. Whippings for the least criticism are common; and Muni suffers such a punishment. He gets a black fellow victim to bend his shackles; a friend gives him $7 and an address. During a bathroom break, he slips away and manages to escape men with rifles and their dogs. He grabs clothing off a line and several times barely escapes detection. He buys a train ticket and while the police chase someone else, he makes it to Chicago. Changing his name to Allen James he works his way up to foreman, surveyor and eventually superintendent. He rents a room from a hard blonde, Glenda Farrell; she wants him to love her, and complains when he tries to study. She learns of his past, and threatens to tell about him unless he marries her. But when he does, she cheats on him with other men and spends his money. He grows interested in another girl, Helen Vinson, and asks Farrell for a divorce. They quarrel and she turns him into the authorities. While awaiting extradition, he tells the press about the chain gangs. The state he escaped from offers to pardon him if he will return serve 90 days voluntarily. He talks to Vinson and decides to go back. But the commission there break their word and refuses to think about pardoning him until he has served a year's sentence, on a chain gang. His brother, a minister, champions his cause but Muni escapes after a year has gone by and there is no end in sight. He uses dynamite in an escape. After finding Vinson to say goodbye, she asks him how he lives. His chilling reply is, "I steal...". The ending is invented; but the filmmakers' purpose was to dramatize the injustice of the punishment system being indicted, all along. The script was adapted by Sheridan Gibney, Brown Holmes and Howard J. Green, from a story serialized and published in several places shortly before the film was made. The details of Burns's life were altered; but the essential spirit of the film is true to the details of his unjust sufferings. The real-life Burns eventually returned to Georgia and without being pardoned was cleared of further charges. The Georgia authorities tried to sue the producers and the film was banned in the state. Director Mervyn LeRoy gave the film a spare B/W documentary look, with plenty of action and intelligent dialogue. The art direction by Jack Okey and the cinematography by Sol Polito are memorably straightforward also. Orry-Kelly is given credit for the gowns used and Berhard Kaun for the terse and effective music. The large supporting cast is filled with familiar names, from Preston Foster and Robert Warwick to Edward Arnold, Lionel Atwill, Everett Brown, Dennis O'Keefe, Jack LaRue, Douglass Dumbrille and Roscoe Karns. Among the more prominent cast members are Sally Blane, Louise Carter, Allen Jenkins, Hale Hamilton, Willard Robinson and many more. Of the leads, Foster is very properly menacing, Farrell is very good as Muni's wife and Vinson sympathetic as his girl. Paul Muni is earnest, never flamboyant and sincere; his rugged and solid performance helps sell the normative nature of the man who is so much more sinned against than sinning. This was merely the first award- nominated look at the injustice of the no-longer American US public interest government's justice system; even though the road gangs were done away with in the late 1930s, but later revived, the theme of the lack of individual justice in many spheres in an increasingly undefined and bureaucratic system==however many honest individuals serve in it--was one taken up often, though not often enough, by Hollywood filmmakers for decades afterward. The genre of hero victims was carried on largely on the strength of the commercial success of this classic expose.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Links.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A World War I veteran (Oscar-nominee Paul Muni) denounces the factory system and falls into poverty and trouble in the deep South. After being wrongly convicted in the robbery of a dining joint, Muni is forced to hard labor on the chain gang. Escape is always on his mind and is finally realized, but his past catches up to him later on in Chicago as he is blackmailed into marriage by a cold and unfeeling woman (Glenda Farrell). When Muni falls in love with Helen Vinson, it seems that Farrell will indeed force our hero back to the brutal chain gangs of the south with horrific results. A social consciousness in the hopes of changing the prison system in the deep South is very prevalent throughout in this film which is based loosely on actual events. Muni, one of the most under-rated and under-appreciated actors the cinema has ever seen, does arguably his greatest work in a complex and heartfelt role. Definitely shades of "All Quiet on the Western Front" as the film goes beyond the medium to convey strong messages and accurate truths associated with social morays of the early 1930s. Thought-provoking and dominant in every way, "I Am a Fugitive From a Chain Gang" continues to be an imperative piece to the American cinema today. 5 stars out of 5.</fullReview>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>More required in the way of digital restoration to get this fugitive out of its chains!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mervyn LeRoy's I Am A Fugitive From A Chain Gang (1932) is a film that Alfred Hitchcock might have enjoyed making, for it develops the wrong man scenario with impeccable panache in vestiges of the human condition turned on its end. The film stars Paul Muni (a largely forgotten actor today, but one of the greatest chameleons on film) as a returning war hero, James Allen. Rejecting his old position at a work house, James soon discovers that other work is hard to come by. Reduced to hobo status, he is accidentally framed from a train robbery and put to work on one of the most ruthless chain gangs. After much suffrage, James manages to make a daring and successful escape to Chicago where he embarks upon transforming himself from unemployed bum into a titan in the construction industry. But reality intervenes his ascendancy when former girl friend, Marie Woods (Glenda Farell) discovers his secret and threatens to expose James to the authorities unless he marries and provides for her own self financial securities. This latter complication is further put to the test when Joe discovers that he has fallen in love with Helen (Helen Vison) instead. In keeping with director, LeRoy's penchant for spinning yarns to the lovelorn, the latter half of this would be gangster and police flick is maudlin melodrama, built up with frothy resistance to the corruption and hard-edged bite of its preceding hour or so.While this film has never looked great on home video, there are marked improvements available for the first time on this Warner DVD. The B&amp;W scale seems more refined than was previously available, with subtler gradations. Flickering of the image is persistent and a tad distracting in some scenes. Age related artifacts abound throughout. There's more than a hint of film grain, and, while I am not adverse to having grain present, there is way too much grain here to suit even my liberal tastes in several key sequences. The audio is mono but nicely balanced. Richard Jewell's commentary is informative. Other extras include a couple of theatrical trailers.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Oslo Jargo (Bartok Kinski) (osloj@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Stale acting and B grade direction and script make film tedious</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is hardly a "classic", nor is it a "social protest movie" that most of these misguided reviewers would have you believe. It falls into the realm of impressionable 'exploitation' films that attempted to substantiate struggling theaters with their wealthy competitors. Warner Bros. looked for "controversial" themes to release them from their box office slump and found it with topics like this, packaging emotional rubbish into the screens in the form of low budget B grade "content films". Anyone who thinks Warner Bros was a "redeeming and caring company interested in the plight of man" is deluded, they were merely out to make a profit, nothing more. Examine their union records, dictatorial management and abuse of rights at the time if you don't believe me. Surely, a film which pulls at your heart is only intended to pull at your wallet.The film itself has a weak script, stale acting (bordering on dull to over anxious), B grade direction, poor dialogue and is tedious. It meanders through one half hearted empty skit after another, dislodging us from any sincere interest at all. Muni is a boorish actor, neither disguising his brusque acting method nor elaborating on any convincing emotion. Standard film shots are scattered throughout, of locations and events in history, a parade from WW 1, a real street scene in Chicago, and this further aggravates the authenticity of the whole subject, making the film look "cut up" because most of it was filmed on cheap Warner Bros sets in Pasadena. The chain gang scenes are unrealistic, exaggerated, and only attempt to garner instant condemnation from the viewer. I don't need a mediocre film to instigate that "chain gangs are evil", I should know that from my own knowledge, and further more, this film contradicts itself because capitalism itself is slavery and there is so much insane dialog about the "virtues of work" in the beginning. I fail to believe that there is a difference between a worker at a factory occupied for 12 hours a day like a slave and a man stuck on a chain gang! An average film reduced by film historians and buffs to a "classic" merely because they can not understand why films had to make a profit for the capitalistic and authoritarian film companies which produced them. Do not tell me about "social awareness" when only profit to powerful monopolistic companies was involved!</fullReview>
